  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_4dde2682-d24a-4d0f-ad70-e902f79d671c'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();

<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I gather that you are reading it as an ebook. Have you tried to re-load the ebook? If self-help tactics don't work, contact your support for help. I h<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I gather that you are reading it as an ebook. Have you tried to re-load the ebook? If self-help tactics don't work, contact your support for help. I have noticed that some of the older readers have had problems especially the ones on Android tablets. My original Kindle lasted over 7 years but I went through 2 Kindle Fire tablets in 3 years because of the Android program. I hope this helps.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<div id="review_2828239164" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2828239164" />
    <a title="Henry Avila" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5431458-henry-avila"><img alt="Henry Avila" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307597403p2/5431458.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2828239164?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Henry Avila" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Henry Avila" href="/user/show/5431458-henry-avila">Henry Avila</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2828239164" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer487266669321941771">This book was published a decade before the real Titanic was discovered 12,500 feet below the surface of the turbulent frigid North Atlantic the most treacherous sea on our planet.The ocean of icebergs and dread, forever associated with the doomed ship sinking into legend. Dirk Pitt is one of those countless millions who are mesmerized by the almost three hundred yards long behemoth sitting on the sand bottom, slowly deteriorating a few scattered pieces of furniture and debris lying by from the </span>
  <span id="freeText487266669321941771" style="display:none">This book was published a decade before the real Titanic was discovered 12,500 feet below the surface of the turbulent frigid North Atlantic the most treacherous sea on our planet.The ocean of icebergs and dread, forever associated with the doomed ship sinking into legend. Dirk Pitt is one of those countless millions who are mesmerized by the almost three hundred yards long behemoth sitting on the sand bottom, slowly deteriorating a few scattered pieces of furniture and debris lying by from the vessel, an eternal purgatory for the once glorious king of the floating liners. Mr. Pitt is a professional, fearless, intelligent, rugged, charismatic not handsome but ordinary looking man the ladies adore. His strict boss Admiral James Sandecker (ret.) the head of the federal agency NUMA, they search and recover shipwrecks, investigate any suspecious activity on the seas, clean deadly spills and on the side eliminate enemies of America. The President has a secret project which will stop Soviet missiles from penetratng U.S. airspace a kind of force field not science fiction but reality. There are always obstacles otherwise it would generate no interest, the C.IA. and Navy will cooperate. A very rare mineral is needed for success and this is where the Titanic is involved the only known quantity is in a safe two and half miles under the sea, yes our ship in the watery grave which never reached her destination New York. Before the revolution a group of nine tough miners from Colorado had after many months in backbreaking effort in the Arctic islands, of Novaya Zemlya, north of Russia they managed to abstract the precious mineral Byzanium and escond with it getting as far as England before their enemies strike hard killing all but one, Joshua Hayes Brewster. He at last is about to get home safely booking passage on the White Star Line ship the Titanic and going with her down. Soviets espionage in Washington reveals a secret salvage operation to steal Byzanium, the mineral is rightfully theirs from its territory. The action becomes quite intense when Russian agents board the ship soon after being raised a sad derelict the once mighty craft. Two traitors help the Soviet invaders capture the boat, the Navy Seals arrive but not for a drink, the rumble begins in earnest. A Dirk Pitt adventure plenty of fun for his admirers the villains are neutralized and our team comes on top, what usually occurs in the series , you thought the bad guys would win? Still the highlight of the expedition is a striptease by the lone woman in the rescue crew for patriotic reasons to distract the enemy she does a fine job. But the doing brings to the numerous loyal readers relief, the man survives again and the world sleeps peacefully, with just a few nightmares.</span>
  <a data-text-id="487266669321941771" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Jennifer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1740039-jennifer"><img alt="Jennifer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491534616p2/1740039.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/143471704?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer" href="/user/show/1740039-jennifer">Jennifer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1740039-jennifer?shelf=author-clive-cussler">author-clive-cussler</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1740039-jennifer?shelf=action-adventure">action-adventure</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer143471704" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer57439606512117701">I think I was in high school when Kate Winslet stood on the bow of the Titanic and let Leonardo DiCaprio take her in his arms as their doomed love affair began onscreen. I saw not only what the Titanic looked like from its final resting place on the bottom of the sea, but also James Cameron's lovingly crafted vision of the Titanic when she sailed on her maiden voyage. This said, Clive Cussler's original imagining of the raising of the Titanic was breathtaking. It was not hard to believe in a wor</span>
  <span id="freeText57439606512117701" style="display:none">I think I was in high school when Kate Winslet stood on the bow of the Titanic and let Leonardo DiCaprio take her in his arms as their doomed love affair began onscreen. I saw not only what the Titanic looked like from its final resting place on the bottom of the sea, but also James Cameron's lovingly crafted vision of the Titanic when she sailed on her maiden voyage. This said, Clive Cussler's original imagining of the raising of the Titanic was breathtaking. It was not hard to believe in a world where even her location on the sea floor was as yet undiscovered. The first discovery of debris from the ship elicited excitement, and the final cresting of her decks above the waves brought tears to my eyes. Cussler gave us an amazing interconnected plot and the best crew in the world to undertake the salvaging of the most amazing ship in "Raise the Titanic!" a decade before she was actually located. Could I go back in a time machine to 1975, I would rather had discovered her with Clive Cussler than James Cameron! (My only caveat to my endorsement is this: The character of Dana Seagram could be completely removed from the book with no qualms from me. She's a 1975 vision of what an 1988 independent working woman and wife would be like, and she is a disappointing, selfish, awful human being, much less, woman. Thank goodness that Mr. Cussler has grown past this view of women in subsequent years.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="57439606512117701" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText2657410988643287203" style="display:none">Raise The Titanic! has been highly recommended to me by a close friend for yeeears and every time he asked if I have read it my response was always - not yet. 😅 If people only understand how many books we GR community have on our TBR!! <div id="review_1163967364" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1163967364" />
    <a title="Scott Rhee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5717792-scott-rhee"><img alt="Scott Rhee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1408223108p2/5717792.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1163967364?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott Rhee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott Rhee" href="/user/show/5717792-scott-rhee">Scott Rhee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5717792-scott-rhee?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5717792-scott-rhee?shelf=clive-cussler">clive-cussler</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5717792-scott-rhee?shelf=action-adventure">action-adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5717792-scott-rhee?shelf=archaeology">archaeology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5717792-scott-rhee?shelf=maritime">maritime</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1163967364" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10512751408400227905">Clive Cussler's novel "Raise the Titanic!" screams manliness. <br/><br/>Written in 1976, "Raise the Titanic!" was the fourth book to feature Cussler's manly hero, Dirk Pitt. Rugged, taciturn, ex-Navy, knows a lot about boats. Also: charmer with the ladies, who don't really figure much in the book. Women have three purposes: secretarial, sexual, or villainous. It's not that Cussler doesn't like women, it's just that he doesn't have a lot of things for them to do, and he's not quite sure what to do with the</span>
  <span id="freeText10512751408400227905" style="display:none">Clive Cussler's novel "Raise the Titanic!" screams manliness. <br /><br />Written in 1976, "Raise the Titanic!" was the fourth book to feature Cussler's manly hero, Dirk Pitt. Rugged, taciturn, ex-Navy, knows a lot about boats. Also: charmer with the ladies, who don't really figure much in the book. Women have three purposes: secretarial, sexual, or villainous. It's not that Cussler doesn't like women, it's just that he doesn't have a lot of things for them to do, and he's not quite sure what to do with them when they're there. Granted, this is Cussler in '76. I'm sure he's evolved somewhat.<br /><br />Sexism aside, Cussler can write a pretty damn good maritime action/adventure yarn. I don't even like boats, and after the first 20 pages or so, I was ready to set sail into a hurricane on a dinghy and man the rigging. (I have no idea what I just wrote there…) Seriously, I still don't have a clue what "port" or "starboard" mean, but it doesn't matter. Cussler's vast knowledge of sea-faring only enhances the pleasure of reading this book, even for land-lubbers like myself.<br /><br />The plot: a rare element known as byzanium is needed in a top-secret government project called The Sicilian Project. It just so happens that, in 1912, a large amount of byzanium was crossing the Atlantic in the cargo hold of a deluxe cruise ship on its maiden voyage. The ship was the Titanic. (Yes, the same ship on which Kate Winslet and Leo DiCaprio met, fell in love, and had sex in a car. According to history, the ship hit an iceberg and sank, never to be found again, until Bill Paxton discovers it many decades later, with funding from James Cameron.) In a series of interesting and somewhat coincidental events, the Titanic is discovered, and a race ensues between the U.S. and the Soviets. Dirk Pitt, a courageous jack-of-all-trades and manly man, leads the expedition to retrieve the Titanic and raise it from its watery grave in order to secure the byzanium. Little things like Soviet assassin spies, hurricanes, and naked women won't get in his way.<br /><br />Did I mention this book is a testosterone-laden manly Manfest?<br /><br />There are some weird things about it. One is that it was published in '76, but it takes place in the "present" of 1987. I'm not sure why Cussler chose to set it in a near future, although I'm sure the fact that the technology for underwater retrieval needed for raising a ship like the Titanic probably wasn't around in '76, or it was still in its early stages. In any case, the 1987 setting threw me off a bit. Interestingly enough, the socio-political issues Cussler writes about in his future aren't that completely far off from the real 1987. Weird.<br /><br />Another weird thing is that, at the time, the Titanic had not been discovered. I can't recall exactly when it was discovered, although I'm pretty sure it was in the early '80s, because I was in elementary school at the time and remember it being in the news. I was fairly certain that scientists would discover that it was full of underwater alien eggs, and that they would hatch and multiply and eventually eat everyone in the world. (I think the movie "Alien" scarred my childhood. I curse my father for letting me stay up late and watch that damn movie…) Anyway, interesting to note that Cussler kind of predicted the discovery of the Titanic. Weird.<br /><br />Overall, I was pretty taken in by "Raise the Titanic!" It's everything a manly techno-thriller should be: exciting, action-packed, full of good ol' fashioned fisticuffs, gunplay, and explosions. And some sexy ladies, of course.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10512751408400227905" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_354371477" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/354371477" />
    <a title="Feliks" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5085729-feliks"><img alt="Feliks" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445015906p2/5085729.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/354371477?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Feliks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Feliks" href="/user/show/5085729-feliks">Feliks</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9780553108880" href="/book/show/84938.Raise_The_Titanic_">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5085729-feliks?shelf=genre-thrillers">genre-thrillers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer354371477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10444468172131649324">The cover blurb really, is not fibbing when it gleefully informs you that this is "Dirk Pitt's greatest adventure!"--for, it truly is. Its a one-of-a-kind conception. A unique reading experience in the annals of all paperback thrillers past, present, and future. <br/><br/>It's surprising to realize that this is (chronologically) not the first 'Dirk Pitt' novel. But its certainly the one which brought his author, Clive Cussler, international fame. Simply stated, Cussler <i>"knocks the ball out of the park"</i> wi</span>
  <span id="freeText10444468172131649324" style="display:none">The cover blurb really, is not fibbing when it gleefully informs you that this is "Dirk Pitt's greatest adventure!"--for, it truly is. Its a one-of-a-kind conception. A unique reading experience in the annals of all paperback thrillers past, present, and future. <br /><br />It's surprising to realize that this is (chronologically) not the first 'Dirk Pitt' novel. But its certainly the one which brought his author, Clive Cussler, international fame. Simply stated, Cussler <i>"knocks the ball out of the park"</i> with this one. I can't recall when a thriller author has ever made such a debut in his first 2-3 books. Maybe Frederick Forsythe? But that was well before my time.<br /><br />Now, as much as I heap scorn on most of the later episodes in the 'Dirk Pitt' story--the unending stream of hackneyed dross which Cussler insists on shoveling into our faces--this is one book where his own lordly opinion of himself, is fully warranted. Sadly, quality quickly peters out after this one--the whole series only has 6-7 books worth glancing at. Cussler's career is one of the grossest examples of a writer 'going downhill' in modern times. Worse than Robert Ludlum in that regard.<br /><br />But in this early effort--Cussler shines. Dazzles! I vividly remember the thrill of just holding that glossy, colorful bookcover in my hands, as he spun his astounding tale. With that cover-art which literally no other book has-ever-had-before, or will-ever-have-again. The massive, spooky afterdeck of the HMS Titanic looming up through the fog and mist. And a story which took readers into a literary region covered by no other novel: espionage mixed with sea-salvage. Its iconic, that paperback cover--audacious, just as is the novel's plot itself. <br /><br />That plot--briefly--is this: the intelligence services of three nations reluctantly realize that the lost-since- 1912 Titanic was carrying a super-secret scientific discovery in its vault. This 'macguffin' is <em>'Beryllium</em>', a mineral to make sophisticated new weapons with; a mineral to tip-the-balance-of-global-power-for-whatever-nation-can-recover-it-first; a mineral ore to kill for. It necessitates first the outlandishly difficult pinpointing of--then, a complicated extrication process upon the doomed ship itself (via giant sacs of air), just to even wrest the secret cargo from its abyssal trench. <br /><br />Down, down, down...there are grim skeletons of trapped passengers wafting to-and-fro in the undersea current; ghostly apparitions, dangerous currents...Cussler is never better than when detailing such fiendishly technical undersea operations. It is a tense sequence here, described with genuine storytelling magic. But this is his <i>forte'.</i> It's what he knows best.<br /><br />Later, when the ship surges up to the surface--there are helicopters and machine guns; ultimatums, standoffs, sabotage, spies; commandos. Clever twists and turns. And then suddenly there's a brilliantly-written scene where everyone--including you--will hold your breath as a gorgeous, leggy brunette strips off her clothes and proudly displays her naked figure (no cries of sexism here, <i>please.</i>). <br /><br />Anyway, what next? Well, I don't rightly know how to describe it but control of the ship goes in the Yanks' favor and then--the ghost ship, with all its lost souls--is impossibly, <i>
  <u>
    <b>towed into New York harbor!!!</b>
  </u>
</i> What? Yes! It's true, I am not even kidding. Can you <em>stand</em> it? I can't friggin' stand it, can you? Greeted by a small-boat armada, crowds lining the docks, claxons and foghorns blaring, fireboats sheeting the air. Right up the Hudson...she makes her destination port at last. It makes you want to leap atop the arms of your chair and <i>roar!</i> Boy, let me tell you what: if this does not make your heart race, or put mist in your eyes--find a defibrillator asap, because you have no pulse. <br /><br />Now, the thing about this novel worth remembering is that 'Titanic-mania' was not always ready-to-hand in our society. It was once a sleeping giant. There was a time when there were no <i>Discovery Channel</i> specials on the topic; and when the public imagination was completely somnolent as to the whole legend. Decades had passed without anyone sparing more than an occasional fleeting thought to the dusty, historic, chestnut that it truly was. Then this book came along, and blew everyone away. So, if anyone today tells you they've been a <em>'Titanic fan all their life'</em>, they're probably fibbing unless they admit the stark, caught-us-unaware paperback market upheaval that this book caused for everyone. It came out of <i>nowhere.</i> Cussler and David ('<em>First Blood</em>') Morrell together, coined the modern action-thriller book genre.<br /><br />The crazy 'Titanic' plot here, introduced readers as well, to an action-figure with an excessively macho and unlikely name. 'Dirk Pitt', husky maritime adventurer. Pitt was 'new', in that we had never seen him before--but old, as in familiar-as-James Bond. Pitt is athletic; hardy; shrewd; and full of quips, as Bond always was. Cussler doesn't break-any-molds with this beefcake. The problem is rather that he never stops re-using that mold..how many novels are there, now? I've lost count.<br /><br />Nevertheless. Even the most discriminating reader of thrillers would be hard put to find a single flaw in this fast-paced book (regardless of how bored we are to become with Pitt in subsequent outings). This one tale, really does offer everything; and its all managed very ably and well. 'Execution'--for once--truly lived up to 'concept' (when one would naturally have every reason to expect that it wouldn't). Pacing, editing, technical details, are all in perfect balance. A delight. As a one-off read, it stands alone and shoulder-to-shoulder <i>with any other single title in its genre.</i> It represents everything that is great in the thriller-genre itself.<br /><br />Its bittersweet now to reflect upon all of this, of course. Can you imagine how fantastic it would have been, had Cussler written more novels as cracking good as this one? But he didn't. That dynamic, 'promise-rich' cover-art doesn't evoke at all, what the bombastic Cussler's follow-through brought to the publishing industry. His later career puts the 'lack' in 'lackluster' (although I'm sure he thought otherwise).<br /><br />But he's wrong. Unfortunately, he wound up spearheading nothing; he barged into our cabin, broke down the door ---only to crash through the flooring and sink like a stone. His mediocre books litter the floor of the thriller genre like the ships he has so long written about--while he laughs all the way to the bank.<br /><br />Ah Cussler...we hardly knew ye. Why couldn't you be straight with us? Was there ever a one-trick-pony that sired so many bastards as you? No, you're not King Poseidon..you're King of Facsimiles and Copycats! You copycatted yourself!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10444468172131649324" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_16331313" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16331313" />
    <a title="Eric" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76688-eric"><img alt="Eric" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1255373698p2/76688.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/16331313?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eric" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eric" href="/user/show/76688-eric">Eric</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">James Bond fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Eric by: </span>
      Lynda Kaemerling
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76688-eric?shelf=action-adventure-thriller">action-adventure-thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer16331313" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13141814990325181095">I decided to read a Dirk Pitt book after noticing that <a href='http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0318649/?ref_=sr_1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sahara</a>, a Matthew McConaughy movie I enjoyed a while back, was based on a literary character. I decided to read this book instead of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41716.Sahara__Dirk_Pitt___11_' title='Sahara (Dirk Pitt, #11) by Clive Cussler' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sahara</a> since I was not familiar with the specific story, but was familiar with the ship. <br/><br/>As for this book, it was interesting although beyond unrealistic at times. It was fairly well written, although I would not call it spellbinding.<br/><br/>I did have a few specific gripes with the book (spoilers follow):<br/><br/>- Although it</span>
  <span id="freeText13141814990325181095" style="display:none">I decided to read a Dirk Pitt book after noticing that <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0318649/?ref_=sr_1" rel="nofollow noopener">Sahara</a>, a Matthew McConaughy movie I enjoyed a while back, was based on a literary character. I decided to read this book instead of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41716.Sahara__Dirk_Pitt___11_" title="Sahara (Dirk Pitt, #11) by Clive Cussler" rel="nofollow noopener">Sahara</a> since I was not familiar with the specific story, but was familiar with the ship. <br /><br />As for this book, it was interesting although beyond unrealistic at times. It was fairly well written, although I would not call it spellbinding.<br /><br />I did have a few specific gripes with the book (spoilers follow):<br /><br />- Although it is a Dirk Pitt adventure, Dirk's character only appears in one paragraph in the first 150 pages. This left me feeling misled by the "Dirk Pitt Adventure" stamp on the cover of the book, although the first 150 pages were interesting, regardless. <br /><br />- I could not stand how Clive Cussler wrote Dana Seagram, the only female character of note in the book. I know this was written in the 1980's, but it was seriously cringe-worthy. <br /><br />- The only character that truly suffers at the end is the one person who sacrificed everything for the good of his country. Even the Russian villain is given a reprieve at the end, but the heroic scientist that saves America loses both his wife and his mind. Not even after Dirk finally finds the macguffin, or when the Sicilian Defense actually works and saves the country, is he revisited. I found this oddly unsatisfying for this type of book.<br /><br />I am now debating whether to pick up another Dirk Pitt book and give it a shot, hoping the flaws I found were specific to this book and not to Clive Cussler.</span>

<div id="review_87215269" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/87215269" />
    <a title="Rob" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2217938-rob"><img alt="Rob" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1306103402p2/2217938.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/87215269?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rob" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rob" href="/user/show/2217938-rob">Rob</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781436208048" href="/book/show/6992195-raise-the-titanic">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2217938-rob?shelf=film-tv-tie-in">film-tv-tie-in</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2217938-rob?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer87215269&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer87215269" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5211413622185903369">Each time I'm tempted to laugh at Cussler, I remind myself that back in '75, <i>Titanic</i> wasn't the world's most over-exposed shipwreck (that would happen ten years later). <br/><br/>My knowledge of reality vs, Cussler's assumptions aside, this yarn goes well enough until, for some reason, Cussler seems to lose his mind. He discards a character he's gotten us to invest ourselves in, insures the happiness of one he hasn't, and generally lets the whole thing take a turn downhill. At the end of the novel, I'm le</span>
  <span id="freeText5211413622185903369" style="display:none">Each time I'm tempted to laugh at Cussler, I remind myself that back in '75, <i>Titanic</i> wasn't the world's most over-exposed shipwreck (that would happen ten years later). <br /><br />My knowledge of reality vs, Cussler's assumptions aside, this yarn goes well enough until, for some reason, Cussler seems to lose his mind. He discards a character he's gotten us to invest ourselves in, insures the happiness of one he hasn't, and generally lets the whole thing take a turn downhill. At the end of the novel, I'm left wondering: why? For the love of heaven, why?<br /><br />I think I have an answer. Cussler is attempting to have his cake and eat it, too. He wants his hero, (note his, not the book's: the hero of this book is Gene Seagram, at least until Cussler pulls the rug out from under him) to be both a man of mystery and the guy everybody loves. The problem with this is, aside from the fact that it will not work—a man of mystery is too remote to be liked, a man who's liked is too well known to be mysterious— is that he's already gotten us to liking Gene Seagram. I think at some point Cussler realizes this and decides to cuckold Seagram, both plot-wise and literally, with Pitt. In the process, Cussler destroys a marriage he's had the reader (this reader, anyway) hoping would make it, sends a man who's sacrificed everything for his country to bedlam, and then packs Pitt off to prove the salvage operation that cost both lives, credulity, and a whopping amount of taxpayer money wasn't necessary in the first place, which would have worked if <i>Titanic</i> had been lost again, but as-is makes no real sense whatsoever. I point out that salvaging <i>Titanic</i> was Seagram's idea, and once again Pitt proves what a loser he is.<br /><br />The only conclusion I can come to is we're supposed to end the book beaming, saying "That Pitt—whatta guy!" as he scampers off into the mist, sunsets being few and far between in merry old England, and Cussler being fond of framing.<br /><br />The funny thing is, Cussler can write when he wants to. The scene of Gene Seagram's near suicide is a nice piece, maybe the best scene in the entire book (although a few of Seagram's other scenes rival it). Of course, it turns out to be useless, since Gene doesn't accomplish a darned thing after that, and then loses his wife and goes crackers to boot. After the lovely advice he gets from Pitt, who can blame him? A bedside manner our ubermench has not.<br /><br />In conclusion: interesting concept, well-done up to a point, then completely goes to hell for obscure reasons best known to the author, although I suspect it's more a burning need to have Pitt be the Best than some half-assed idea that <i>Titanic</i> is cursed. All I'm left deciding is whether or not to read another outing of the Pittster. I'll have to sleep on it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5211413622185903369" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Bob Mayer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4460714-bob-mayer"><img alt="Bob Mayer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597686468p2/4460714.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13207141847565997767">The book that broke Clive Cussler out. We now know, of course, that what was proposed was an impossibility given the state of the ship, but we didn’t know it then. The story I heard years ago was that a copy of manuscript was lying on an editor’s desk and someone walking by saw the title and was intrigued and the buzz started. One thing I constantly teach is that title is the #1 marketing tool you have control over as an author.<br/><br/>That’s on top of the fact it was a great idea. RIP Clive.<br/></span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2627289784" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2627289784" />
    <a title="Chris Gager" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5295008-chris-gager"><img alt="Chris Gager" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307204529p2/5295008.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2627289784?book_show_action=true">Dec 15, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Gager" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Gager" href="/user/show/5295008-chris-gager">Chris Gager</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9780553108880" href="/book/show/84938.Raise_The_Titanic_">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2627289784" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11304340997462914283">I needed to finish off my evening reading last night with some mental chewing gum and this is what I picked off my shelves. So far so good and easy on the brain after Dostoevski and Sterne. The dialogue is at best functional, but the plot is all in a book like this anyway, and it's a page-turner so far. The Colorado stuff is appealing. Mr. C. lived in Arvada, I think.<br/><br/>- plenty of product placement so far, and lots of smokers smoking pipes, cigars, and cigarettes.<br/><br/>Moving right along with this engro</span>
  <span id="freeText11304340997462914283" style="display:none">I needed to finish off my evening reading last night with some mental chewing gum and this is what I picked off my shelves. So far so good and easy on the brain after Dostoevski and Sterne. The dialogue is at best functional, but the plot is all in a book like this anyway, and it's a page-turner so far. The Colorado stuff is appealing. Mr. C. lived in Arvada, I think.<br /><br />- plenty of product placement so far, and lots of smokers smoking pipes, cigars, and cigarettes.<br /><br />Moving right along with this engrossing read. I am undeterred by the ridiculously dated sexist take on women in this book. Other Goodreads reviewers have commented on it. Makes me shake my head and laugh and move on.<br /><br />- "Bronco" Stadium???? NO! It's(or was) Mile High Stadium.<br /><br />- Poor Dana - the author makes us hate her already, because she's a married woman who's poor frazzled husband is trying to save the nation while she frets about not getting laid(by another man) when she wants to.<br /><br />- The back cover picture is inaccurate. The iceberg did NOT rip a lateral hole in the hull of the Titanic. It buckled the hull plates, which caused water to come rushing in along the buckled area.<br /><br />Didn't do much book reading last night between listening to football and perusing a bunch of magazines. One thing did become clear, however, and that is that other(mostly female) G'reads reviewers have it right when they cite the author for his relentless, gratuitous male chauvinist piggery. The relentless women-as-sexual-objects thing is in fact rather striking. Also quite dated ... but, the plot is all and it keeps one interested.<br /><br />Moving along now as the BIG project is underway with as many serious complications as CC can toss into the pot. Seems unlikely that Pitt would be told to back off on pursuing the Russian saboteurs, but what do I know. This IS fiction. BTW the back illustration now makes sense - sort of - but there's still no reason why the hull plates above the water line along the "gash" should be depicted curled outward. Anyway, we now know that there was no gash in real life. Also, of course, we now know that the ship went down and settled in two pieces, not one, which CC didn't know when he wrote this.<br /><br />- Dirk's in Exeter, Dawlish and Teignmouth. I visited those places on a bicycle trip back in the summer of 1980. Train from London to Exeter and bicycle to Plymouth. Very scenic indeed!<br /><br />Finished up last night with a whirlwind of well-written if implausible action stuff. What WAS actually plausible was the method that Dirk Pitt used to save the day. Perfectly logical if you think about it. Then came a gratuitous sex scene(REALLY!?!?) with super-pukey dialogue, some political exposition(of the author's views, I assume), a bit of spy vs spy silliness, and a couple of false endings before we wind up with Dirk in a cemetery in Norfolk(I think). Shades of "The Nine Tailors"!<br /><br />- What kind of coffee? "Steaming mugs" of it - OF COURSE! A dead giveaway of bad writing.<br /><br />- Barely a 2.75* - rounds up to 3*</span>
  <a data-text-id="11304340997462914283" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3975694362027073415">I've always been a fan of the Dirk Pitt novels. I read them when I was in my 20's and loved them. Back then, I would have given all of them 5 stars. Now that I've read far more books in a lot of different genres, I'm more critical. I'm re-reading through these novels, and up to this one have been a bit more disappointed than I am with #4. I remember liking this one a lot more than the others 20 years ago as well. The plotting is more intricate, and Pitt's character is more developed, showing a s</span>
  <span id="freeText3975694362027073415" style="display:none">I've always been a fan of the Dirk Pitt novels. I read them when I was in my 20's and loved them. Back then, I would have given all of them 5 stars. Now that I've read far more books in a lot of different genres, I'm more critical. I'm re-reading through these novels, and up to this one have been a bit more disappointed than I am with #4. I remember liking this one a lot more than the others 20 years ago as well. The plotting is more intricate, and Pitt's character is more developed, showing a side that he hasn't in the first 3 books. The subject matter, the Titanic, is well researched and presented well for the time it was written. We know more now, of course, which dates some aspects of this book. But a great story, and well worth reading for any Cussler fan.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer18145675275623489209">This was my first taste of Clive Cussler. I read this in 1984 as a fourteen year old. As you can imagine I fell hopelessly in love with Dirk Pitt and I still AM. LOL, My Father was a huge Cussler Fan and had every book. I choose this one first because it was about the Titanic (and I love Titanic stuff) after I finished this I read everything by him in a month, my father was very proud of me and we had the most wonderful discussions on every book. <br/><br/>C.Cussler's writing is fantastic as a stand ALONE</span>
  <span id="freeText18145675275623489209" style="display:none">This was my first taste of Clive Cussler. I read this in 1984 as a fourteen year old. As you can imagine I fell hopelessly in love with Dirk Pitt and I still AM. LOL, My Father was a huge Cussler Fan and had every book. I choose this one first because it was about the Titanic (and I love Titanic stuff) after I finished this I read everything by him in a month, my father was very proud of me and we had the most wonderful discussions on every book. <br /><br />C.Cussler's writing is fantastic as a stand ALONE author,I could not read his new co-written works, I put both down unfinished which is unheard off with a Cussler, I just could NOT get into them, to the point I re-read my old Cussler's when I need my Dirk Fix LOL.<br /><br /> I've traveled to many places in many time lines due to the historical incidents which are the catalyst for the adventure stories he writes about.Even talking about a Dirk Pitt adventure is exciting.<br /><br />I describe Dirk Pitt as the Underwater 'James Bond'.<br /><br />The film of this,is just too awful to watch, unlike Sahara, I think they done a great job with that and was well cast but only because M.McC is a major Dirk Pitt Fan which is totally understandable.<br /><br /><br />Read ANY Clive Cussler Novel, they are all 5 stars EXCEPT the co-written stuff....sorry but if it ain't broken....Don't fix it !<br /><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_308933668" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Jim Razinha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5963979-jim-razinha"><img alt="Jim Razinha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1312509642p2/5963979.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer308933668" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16619566974194410419">Context is everything...and should probably be considered when re-reading early Cussler books. He might have thought he was crafting a strong female character, must he was sexist through and through in attempting it. Think 20 something male and a culture of Hugh Hefner in the 70s with an underwater James Bond who never fails to get the girl, even in the most remote environments and you'll get the picture. <br/><br/>As this was just his third novel, Cussler had yet to perfect his maddening habit of having </span>
  <span id="freeText16619566974194410419" style="display:none">Context is everything...and should probably be considered when re-reading early Cussler books. He might have thought he was crafting a strong female character, must he was sexist through and through in attempting it. Think 20 something male and a culture of Hugh Hefner in the 70s with an underwater James Bond who never fails to get the girl, even in the most remote environments and you'll get the picture. <br /><br />As this was just his third novel, Cussler had yet to perfect his maddening habit of having his characters voice the things that most people would just think to themselves, but the seeds were here. I must not have noticed thirty years ago that he called the military head of the Navy the "Navy Chief of Staff" - it's "Chief of Naval Operations"...the other is an Army or Air Force term - odd mistake for someone so wrapped up in the sea in real life.<br /><br />Despite his characters being caricatures and his plots formulaic, for whatever reason, I like his books. I still have a fascination with <i>Titanic</i>, due in some part to this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16619566974194410419" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13738526439441242699">I have avoided Clive Cussler like the plague due to him being one of two authors he tried desperately to push on me and my brother growing up.   <span id="freeText13738526439441242699" style="display:none">I have avoided Clive Cussler like the plague due to him being one of two authors he tried desperately to push on me and my brother growing up. He swore by Dirk Pitt as the symbol of adventure and patriotism and I tell you I never knew one day I'd pick up the book and be writing a review like this.<br /><br />I was pleasantly surprised by how this book turned out. What started out for me as a slow and overly political book about spies and Russians and Americans and espionage turned into a very intense experience.<br /><br />My heart raced during their trials in bringing such a tragic and beautiful ship to the shores and Dirk Pitt was a quick witted smart alec who you don't know if you really like but you certaintly can't bring yourself to hate.<br /><br />I found myself most connected to the many aray of secondary characters who all varied and had the same in depth character development that I am unsure how Cussler kept up with so many tragic lives. <br /><br />I definitely see myself looking into another Dirk Pitt adventure in the future. Kudos, mister Cussler.</span>

<div id="review_1717060634" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1717060634" />
    <a title="Glen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20069595-glen"><img alt="Glen" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1717060634?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glen" href="/user/show/20069595-glen">Glen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20069595-glen?shelf=maritime">maritime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20069595-glen?shelf=men-s-adventure">men-s-adventure</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1717060634" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5736277898632613055">I've been reading Cussler for the last couple of years, but this book, the one that sealed his name as a best seller is the earliest one I've read.<br/><br/>It's very different than the ones I've read. Instead of a formulaic novel with an insane billionaire and an end of the world device, we get a deep cold war thriller. The search is on for a rare earth element that just happened to be on the Titanic. None of the moral equivalence that was so prevalent then.<br/><br/>Very good. Highly recommended. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2059251610" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2059251610" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9698647-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1341102729p2/9698647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2059251610?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/9698647-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671725198" href="/book/show/1993498.Raise_The_Titanic_">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9698647-matt?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9698647-matt?shelf=2019-reads">2019-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2059251610" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15640342280858370121">Deep in the North Atlantic is the rarest radioactive mineral on the planet that can power a strategically redefining missile defense shield, the problem is that it’s in vault of the most famous shipwreck in history. <i>Raise the Titanic!</i> is the third published book of Clive Cussler’s series featuring Dirk Pitt, who accepts the challenge to bring the most famous shipwreck in history only to find himself in the middle of a shadowy Cold War encounter in the middle of a hurricane.<br/><br/>A secret U.S. governme</span>
  <span id="freeText15640342280858370121" style="display:none">Deep in the North Atlantic is the rarest radioactive mineral on the planet that can power a strategically redefining missile defense shield, the problem is that it’s in vault of the most famous shipwreck in history. <i>Raise the Titanic!</i> is the third published book of Clive Cussler’s series featuring Dirk Pitt, who accepts the challenge to bring the most famous shipwreck in history only to find himself in the middle of a shadowy Cold War encounter in the middle of a hurricane.<br /><br />A secret U.S. government think tank, Meta Section, sends a mineralogist to Novaya Zemlya in the Soviet Union to look for byzanium that will power their new antiballistic missile defense system. Unfortunately the scientist is discovered by Soviet security until he’s saved by Dirk Pitt whose “interference” of Meta Section’s plan upsets its chief, Dr. Gene Seagram who is married to a NUMA colleague of Pitt’s. The mineralogist how has both good and bad news, Novaya Zemlya does have byzanium but only a tiny fraction that did because it was mined earlier in the century by Americans from Colorado. Gene Seagram and his closest friend in Meta Section begin hunting down this new lead while Captain Prevlov of Soviet Naval Intelligence begins investigating the American incursion of Soviet territory both the recent and newly discovered mine from the turn of the century. Meanwhile Gene Seagram’s quest to get his secret project completed results in his wife Dana leaving him just as he learns that large amount of byzanium was found on Novaya Zemlya by Coloradan miners who spirited from Russia to Britain and loaded it on the Titanic. Gene Seagram finds Pitt and convinces him to lead the salvaging of the shipwreck. Over the course of the salvage, both American and Soviet intelligence agencies have a shadowy back and forth that Pitt only learns about close to the end of salvage which comes to a dramatic end when they raise it to save one of their submersibles. In preparing to tow the now risen Titanic, Pitt and crew learn that a mid-May formed hurricane is barreling towards them which for Prevlov is prefect for his plan to steal the ship and its “secret” cargo. Prevlov and his strike force board the Titanic while it’s in the eye of the hurricane and take the crew hostage, save Pitt, who confronts the Soviet captain before several minutes before Navy SEALS take out the Soviet soldiers except for Prevlov. The Titanic makes arrives in New York to fanfare, but Meta Section and NUMA learn that their efforts were for not because in the ship’s vault were only boxes of normal stones. Gene Seagram, who had slowly been losing his sanity, has a complete nervous breakdown and attacks the mummified remains of the last American miner who by coincidence was also insane by the time he boarded Titanic. Pitt travels to Scotland and makes his way across Britain until he finds a tiny village where one of the American miners was buried, with the bzyanium thus allowing the U.S. to create its missile defense system.<br /><br />Written almost a decade before the Titanic was discovered, in two pieces, Cussler writes an intriguing narrative of underwater discovery and salvage with some nice Cold War intrigue thrown in. The main plot was basically really fun to read even with the knowledge that Cussler’s details were wrong in every regard to the shipwreck. While the Soviet spy subplot was completely fine as was Gene Seagram’s slow mental breakdown, the other subplots in the book were complete trash. First was Dana Seagram’s independent woman angle in which was asserted herself and also bashed women’s liberation (which went hand on hand with the usual chauvinistic streak of these early books), the second was the President of the United States being persuaded by the CIA and NIA to let Meta Section’s secret project get leaked to the Soviets which was completely unrealistic (even in a story about raising the Titanic!). Even with those I would have been fine, but Cussler for some reason had Pitt bed Dana Seagram after going the majority of the book seemingly like the Pitt later in the series.<br /><br /><i>Raise the Titanic!</i> for the most part is a good book, which could have been better, but it’s better than <i>The Mediterranean Caper</i> and <i>Iceberg</i> especially since Dirk isn’t a complete jerk. Cussler did write a solid main plot, but the biggest problems were some of the subplots which undermined the work plus some poor decisions around Pitt close to the end. However, after <i>Pacific Vortex</i> this is the best early Pitt book so far.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15640342280858370121" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1628478952" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1628478952" />
    <a title="Kirsten Simkiss" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16550352-kirsten-simkiss"><img alt="Kirsten Simkiss" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540600215p2/16550352.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1628478952?book_show_action=true">Jun 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kirsten Simkiss" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kirsten Simkiss" href="/user/show/16550352-kirsten-simkiss">Kirsten Simkiss</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671725198" href="/book/show/1993498.Raise_The_Titanic_">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16550352-kirsten-simkiss?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1628478952" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13134459057088251018">You know, I was really hoping I'd be less disappointed in this book than the second book, and I guess I was to an extent. But the toxic masculinity is still there, even if it only reared it's ugly head a few times. I think this book was greatly helped by the fact that the main character, Dirk Pitt, was only in about half the book. It made it more bearable for me since he's such a total douchebag. I'm just not at all impressed with him. They keep saying he's good at his job, but I'm waiting to se</span>
  <span id="freeText13134459057088251018" style="display:none">You know, I was really hoping I'd be less disappointed in this book than the second book, and I guess I was to an extent. But the toxic masculinity is still there, even if it only reared it's ugly head a few times. I think this book was greatly helped by the fact that the main character, Dirk Pitt, was only in about half the book. It made it more bearable for me since he's such a total douchebag. I'm just not at all impressed with him. They keep saying he's good at his job, but I'm waiting to see the actual evidence of it. All he seems to be good at is treating women like trash.<br /><br />I'd pay good money for a book where all the women he's bedded and discarded and insulted casually behind their backs come back to fight him. The man is scum.<br /><br />As far as the adventure of it goes, I had some fairly minor complaints. The mystery of it was great - the Titanic is such a beloved topic by so many. As a line of events in a story, it's a pretty interesting thing. However, with all the frankly bad science and inaccuracies about the Titanic - including information that was well known about the condition of the Titanic that was conveniently ignored by Cussler - it really killed it for me. Plus, with the USA now free of the fear of Nuclear Missile attacks at the end of the book, I feel like there should be an arms race from hell. However, it likely will never get mentioned because it's such a stupidly overpowered defense system.<br /><br />Between the poor treatment of his sole female character as essentially a sexy lamp for people to obsess over and the lack of actual realism in the science, this really just isn't that impressive as a book. I don't recommend it. I especially don't recommend it if you're a nerd about the Titanic.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13134459057088251018" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/222676583?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Todd Russell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Todd Russell" href="/user/show/5198301-todd-russell">Todd Russell</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5198301-todd-russell?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer222676583" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3304774654790542758">When I was younger I enjoyed reading the James Bond books and Clive Cussler's Dirk Pitt is sort of like James Bond in the Navy. He's got the attitude and charisma with the ladies anyway. Raise the Titanic is an exciting read for fans of the Titanic. The story is written to happen in 1988 but was actually written in the 70s. <br/><br/>There is valuable material buried in a vault inside the Titanic that the United States wants and spends hundreds of millions to have a team of specialists led by NUMA man Dir</span>
  <span id="freeText3304774654790542758" style="display:none">When I was younger I enjoyed reading the James Bond books and Clive Cussler's Dirk Pitt is sort of like James Bond in the Navy. He's got the attitude and charisma with the ladies anyway. Raise the Titanic is an exciting read for fans of the Titanic. The story is written to happen in 1988 but was actually written in the 70s. <br /><br />There is valuable material buried in a vault inside the Titanic that the United States wants and spends hundreds of millions to have a team of specialists led by NUMA man Dirk Pitt raise the Titanic. Meanwhile, the Russians smell something is up and try to intervene.<br /><br />What makes this story thrilling to me wasn't only the action scenes but the detailed description of how they plan to raise the Titanic. There are several interesting chapters on the techniques and Cussler weaves this information in with a love story and some conflicts between a few characters. Those like me who enjoy the alluring history of the Titanic and thrillers at sea will enjoy this read.<br /><br />More Dirk Pitt! </span>
  <a data-text-id="3304774654790542758" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/334225-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer109032431" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11842845915778332681">I saw the movie "Raise the Titanic" way back when I was a little kid and had a fondness for the movie (which I suspect was a <i>really, really bad</i> movie given that it disappeared quickly and has never been seen since). Mostly I remember the Titanic surfacing and thinking "wow, that was cool." So I thought the book would be fun, too.<br/><br/>It's not. It's awful-terrible-horrible even by my standards for thrillers, which can be pretty good even when they're pretty bad. Now, I love a good thriller, but I had </span>
  <span id="freeText11842845915778332681" style="display:none">I saw the movie "Raise the Titanic" way back when I was a little kid and had a fondness for the movie (which I suspect was a <i>really, really bad</i> movie given that it disappeared quickly and has never been seen since). Mostly I remember the Titanic surfacing and thinking "wow, that was cool." So I thought the book would be fun, too.<br /><br />It's not. It's awful-terrible-horrible even by my standards for thrillers, which can be pretty good even when they're pretty bad. Now, I love a good thriller, but I had a hard time finishing this. It has paper-thin characters, cartoon bad guys, the completely random (yet boring) sex scene with the hero that makes no sense at all, repeated contrived near-death situations, and worst of all, the fun part -- raising the Titanic -- is finished pretty quickly.<br /><br />I miss my Tom Clancy.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_589247895" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/589247895" />
    <a title="Ross Armstrong" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13097818-ross-armstrong"><img alt="Ross Armstrong" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1364385022p2/13097818.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/589247895?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ross Armstrong" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ross Armstrong" href="/user/show/13097818-ross-armstrong">Ross Armstrong</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9780553108880" href="/book/show/84938.Raise_The_Titanic_">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13097818-ross-armstrong?shelf=action-adventure">action-adventure</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer589247895" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6177369962456845284">This wasn't the first Dirk Pitt novel, but it was the one that firmly established Cussler's formula. The story opens aboard the Titanic in 1912. A mystery is set up. Move to the near future. (Yes, the future. The book was published in 1976 but set in 1987.)<br/>The U.S. is working on a Star Wars style missile defense program but the only power source strong enough is a rare mineral that can only be found on a Russian owned island. Dirk Pitt is sent to investigate and discovers that the ore was mined </span>
  <span id="freeText6177369962456845284" style="display:none">This wasn't the first Dirk Pitt novel, but it was the one that firmly established Cussler's formula. The story opens aboard the Titanic in 1912. A mystery is set up. Move to the near future. (Yes, the future. The book was published in 1976 but set in 1987.)<br />The U.S. is working on a Star Wars style missile defense program but the only power source strong enough is a rare mineral that can only be found on a Russian owned island. Dirk Pitt is sent to investigate and discovers that the ore was mined out back in 1912 and put aboard the Titanic for shipment to the U.S.<br />NUMA under Admiral Sandecker organizes a massive recovery effort to Raise The Titanic led by Dirk Pitt.<br /><br />The book is dated. It was written before the Titanic was located and Cussler did not know that the remains were in two main sections. In the novel, the Titanic is intact on the bottom. Still, this is a fun Cold War espionage thriller with lots of action and intrigue.<br /><br />This has been turned into a movie. The movie was a lesson in how not to adapt a novel. While it had a decent budget and the underwater visuals were well done, it turned Admiral Sandecker, a major SUPPORTING character into the MAIN character and Dirk Pitt is turned into a SUPPORTING CHARACTER!!!<br />This is equivalent to making a Bond film and having M as the lead not Bond. The film also introduced a plotline not in the novel about using the mineral for a bomb. Also, in the book there is a major action set-piece aboard the Titanic after it has been raised. In the film this is reduced to a sub surfacing and a flyover by a couple of F-14's.<br /><br /><br />I really enjoyed the book but was extremely disappointed in the movie.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6177369962456845284" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2187840600492654103">My first read in the Dirk Pitt adventure series. What a story! A brilliant twist on the story of the ill fated Titanic. Excellent prelude. Plot was built and always kept me guessing and wondering where it was going. Then with a few more pages of reading the answer became apparent over and over again.   <span id="freeText2187840600492654103" style="display:none">My first read in the Dirk Pitt adventure series. What a story! A brilliant twist on the story of the ill fated Titanic. Excellent prelude. Plot was built and always kept me guessing and wondering where it was going. Then with a few more pages of reading the answer became apparent over and over again. From espionage, marinetime salvaging, political conflicts, a disturbing love affair, brutal hurricanes and sabotage is an arms race between two super powers. The prize for what is thought to be on the Titanic is the ultimate weapon.<br />With a plot that twists and corckscrews through out i have to say this book really is spell binding. Only draw back for me was some dull reading near the end and perhaps slighty too short with the Golden story that clive cussler has drawn from the edge of his imagination. Plus side is the Titanic really is paid huge credit as a mighty ship who holds her own and captivates and demands everyones respect. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16482906830129689322">I should have probably done my research before trying to read this.<br/><br/>You see, i am doing reasearch for my own novel based on the decks of the doomed liner.   <span id="freeText16482906830129689322" style="display:none">I should have probably done my research before trying to read this.<br /><br />You see, i am doing reasearch for my own novel based on the decks of the doomed liner. This book came up in my googling, so i bought it. However, what i didn't know was this was published in 1980. Five years before the Titanic's wreck was discovered.<br /><br />There was also a lot of creative liscence taken with this story (or i hope, otherwise Cussler should have really done his reasearch more furrowly) , so that makes it practically useless for research purposes.<br /><br />However what really killed this story for me was the dialogue. The characters talk like writers writing a scene, i find it very unbelievable that people would actually say some of these things in general conversation. They just killed it for me. </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2321648187" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6012362526409113525">A highly entertaining 1970's Cold War thriller starring Dirk Pitt.<br/><br/>It's been discovered that a rare radioactive material 'Byzantium' was being transported to American on the Titanic. It's down to Pitt and the team to try and salvage the wreckage. <br/>Whilst at the same time the Russian's want to get their hands on the rare isotope.<br/><br/>The plot sounds bonkers, but it really works. It's a highly enjoyable blockbuster novel (I can see why they adapted it for a movie - I really want to watch it now!)<br/><br/>This was</span>
  <span id="freeText6012362526409113525" style="display:none">A highly entertaining 1970's Cold War thriller starring Dirk Pitt.<br /><br />It's been discovered that a rare radioactive material 'Byzantium' was being transported to American on the Titanic. It's down to Pitt and the team to try and salvage the wreckage. <br />Whilst at the same time the Russian's want to get their hands on the rare isotope.<br /><br />The plot sounds bonkers, but it really works. It's a highly enjoyable blockbuster novel (I can see why they adapted it for a movie - I really want to watch it now!)<br /><br />This was the fourth book that I've read in this series, I'm really starting to get hooked on these now.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13292156854916884713">Good book, but certainly not my favorite in the Dirk Pitt canon. I think Iceberg was better than this one.<br/><br/>But this one made Clive Cussler famous, and all those expensive cars on the back covers possible.<br/><br/>So you should read it. And know that this series gets a whole lot better, the action more intense, and it all eventually looks like raising the Titanic was child's play. <br/><br/>Yeah, saving the world ain't a walk in the park, folks!</span>

          </span>
